DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -4 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  Section J: Safety and Exposure 
Prevention  
As the employer, Franchisees are responsible for the job safety of their 
Team Members, especially the Nurses. The implementation of the 
Occupational Health and Safety (OHS)  standards is critical for ensuring 
Nurses' safety in The DRIPBaR locations, where they are at risk of exposure 
to bloodborne pathogens and infectious diseases.  You must confirm and 
comply with the OHS regulations in your respective province or territory. 
What we have outlined is best practice but may differ in your area.  
OHS Regulations  
OHS regulations regarding  bloodborne pathogens have  placed specific 
responsibilities on  employers for the protection of  employees. These  include 
all the following:  
1. Identifying positions or tasks covered by the bloodborne and other 
potentially infectious materials (OPIM) standard precautions.  
2. Creating an Exposure Control Plan to minimize the possibility of 
exposure and making the plan easily accessible to  all employees.  
3. Developing and putting into action a written schedule for cleaning and 
decontaminating work  environment/surfaces.  
4. Creating a system for easy identification of soiled material and its 
proper disposal.  
5. Developing a system for annual training for all covered employees.  
6. Offering the opportunity for employees to get the Hepatitis B 
vaccination at no cost.  
7. Establishing clear procedures to follow for reporting an exposure.  
8. Creating a system of recordkeeping.  
9. In workplaces where there is potential exposure to injuries from 
contaminated sharps, soliciting input from non -managerial employees 
with potential exposure regarding the identification, evaluation, and 
selection of effective  engineering and work practice controls (feedback 
of those being exposed).  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -5 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  10. If a needlestick injury occurs, record the appropriate information in 
the Sharps Injury Log , including  the type/brand  of device involved, 
location of the incident, and description of the incident.  
11. Maintaining a Sharps Injury Log  in such a way that protects the 
privacy of employees.  
12. Ensuring the confidentiality of all employees' medical records and 
exposure incidents.  
Exposure Control Plan  
OHS's Bloodborne Pathogens Standard requires the implementation of an 
Exposure Control Plan (ECP). This includes the use of personal protective 
equipment (PPE), such as gloves and gowns, and procedures for the safe 
disposal of sharps and other medical waste. Employees covered by the 
bloodborne pathogens standard receive an explanation of this ECP during 
their Initial Training session. It will also be reviewed in their annual refresher 
training.  Employers are to have mandatory training of staff on safe injec tion, 
and sharps handling procedures.  
Employers must update the plan annually to reflect changes in 
tasks,  procedures, and positions that affect occupational exposure; updates 
should also take into account technological changes that eliminate or reduce 
occupational exposure.  
In addition, employers must annually document in the plan that they 
have considered and have begun using appropriate, commercially available, 
effectively  safer medical devices designed to eliminate or minimize 
occupational  exposure.  
Employers must also document that they have solicited input from 
frontline  workers in identifying, evaluating, and selecting effective 
engineering and  work practice controls.  
While details may vary from one employer to the next, every 
relevant  workplace must provide easily accessible copies of this plan to its 
employees. Each ECP must include:  
1. A determination of exposure by job classification  
2. The implementation of various methods of exposure control, including:  
a. Universal or Standard Precautions  
b. Engineering and Work Practice Controls  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -6 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  c. Personal Protective Equipment  
d. Information on the Hepatitis B vaccine  
e. Communication of hazards to employees and required training 
(use of signs  and labels)  
f. Recordkeeping  
g. Procedures for evaluating circumstances surrounding exposure 
incidents  
h. Post-exposure evaluation and follow -up 
3. Implementation of methods for all the above  
Exposure Risk from Bloodborne Pathogens and 
Infectious Diseases  
Nurses providing IV therapies face an increased risk for disease transmission 
due to the nature of their work environment where the vitamins are 
administered intravenously, necessitating close contact with client s and 
exposure to bloodborne pathogens, which can increase the likelihood of 
transmission if proper protocols are not strictly followed. Therefore, The 
DRIPBaR Nurses must be trained in infection control techniques, including 
handwashing, the use of PPE, s afe handling and disposal of sharps, and 
rigorous sanitization procedures to maintain a safe environment for 
themselves and their clients. Nurses must be trained in these procedures 
and the proper handling of potentially infectious materials, even if they have 
been previous ly trained. 
Workers exposed to bloodborne pathogens and infectious diseases are at 
risk for serious life -threatening  illnesses. OHS's Bloodborne Pathogens 
Standard prescribes safeguards to protect  workers against health hazards 
from exposure to blood and other potentially  infectious materials and to 
reduce their risk from this exposure.  Comply with provincial OHS codes for 
bloodborne pathogen safety and infection control.  
Not all bloodborne pathogens and infectious diseases are created equally, as  some 
can produce mild symptoms, while others can be life -threatening.  A bloodborne 
pathogen is a microorganism that is present in human blood and can  cause 
disease in humans . An infectious disease is a disease (also caused by 
microorganisms like bacteria, viruses,  fungi, etc.) that enters the body 
through various biological routes.  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -7 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  How Bloodborne Pathogens Spread  
Bloodborne pathogens are transmitted by various routes:  
‚Ä¢ Direct contact transmission occurs when microorganisms are 
transferred from one infected person to another person. For example, 
a tattoo artist with an open, uncovered cut or wound  could come in 
contact with blood from a client/source . 
‚Ä¢ Parenteral exposure is when infected blood and/or bodily fluids are 
introduced into the body through piercing or puncturing the skin. An 
example would be getting stuck with a contaminated needle or being 
cut with a sharp object that has been contaminated.  
‚Ä¢ Indirect contact is when a contaminated object (tools, needles, etc.) 
makes contact with a person's skin or mucous membranes, like those 
found in the eyes, mouth, nose, and ears. This is why it is so 
important to decontaminate any objects that have blood o n them.  
‚Ä¢ Airborne transmission occurs when droplets or small particles that 
contain an infectious agent remain active in the air and are then 
inhaled into the body. An example of this would be tuberculosis. While 
airborne transmission is possible, most cases of blo odborne pathogen 
infections do not fall into this category.  
The primary source of potential bloodborne pathogens is blood and specific 
bodily fluids.  Potentially infectious bodily fluids include:  
‚Ä¢ Semen  
‚Ä¢ Vaginal Secretions  
‚Ä¢ Cerebrospinal Fluid  
‚Ä¢ Synovial Fluid  
‚Ä¢ Pleural Fluid  
‚Ä¢ Peritoneal Fluid  
‚Ä¢ Amniotic Fluid  
‚Ä¢ Saliva (in dental procedures)  
‚Ä¢ Any bodily fluid that is visibly contaminated with blood  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -8 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  Levels of Potential Risk  
Nurse training and refreshers keep the risk of transmission top of mind and 
encourage vigilance in implementing the ECP.  
‚Ä¢ Highest Potential Risk ‚Äî The most common ways bloodborne 
pathogens and OPIM are spread are:  
o Getting stuck with an infected needle  
o Sexual contact  
o Other than sexual contact, the highest potential risks are when a 
contaminated sharp object punctures or cuts the skin, such as 
an infected needle, a broken piece of contaminated glass, or a 
razor used by someone else . 
‚Ä¢ Medium Potential Risk: Medium risks involve situations where blood 
and bodily fluids get into an open cut or are absorbed through a 
mucous membrane ‚Äî eyes, nose, ears, mouth, etc.  
‚Ä¢ Lowest Potential Risk: The lowest potential risks include situations 
where contaminated objects come in contact with inflamed skin, acne, 
skin abrasions, etc.  
Types of Bloodborne Diseases  
Hepatitis B (HBV)  
The virus reproduces in the liver, which causes inflammation that leads 
to cirrhosis of the liver, liver cancer, and liver failure. The Hepatitis B virus is 
very durable, and it can survive in dried blood for up to seven days.  
When first infected, Hepatitis B begins as an  acute infection (meaning short 
in duration)  that can range from very mild conditions with few or no 
symptoms to a  serious condition requiring hospitalization.  
Approximately 90% of those infected (adults and older children) will be able 
to fight  the virus and expel it from their bodies within a few months 
and subsequently develop an immunity to it; 10% of people who contract 
Hepatitis B will develop  a chronic condition.  
 
 
 

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -9 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  How is HBV Contracted?  
The workplace risk is from accidental puncture by contaminated needles and 
other sharps which can  result in the transmission of bloodborne pathogens.  
Other means of transmission are:  
‚Ä¢ Hepatitis B is mainly spread through sexual contact with an 
infected  person or as a result of sharing needles or syringes with 
an infected  person.  
‚Ä¢ The infection can also be passed from a mother to an unborn (or just -
born) baby, especially if the infant comes into contact with blood 
or other bodily fluids through breaks in the skin like cuts and sores.  
HBV Signs and Symptoms  
Hepatitis B symptoms include, but are not limited to:  
‚Ä¢ Yellow skin (jaundice)  
‚Ä¢ Yellowing eyes  
‚Ä¢ Tiredness and fatigue  
‚Ä¢ Loss of appetite  
‚Ä¢ Nausea  
‚Ä¢ Dark urine  
‚Ä¢ Joint pain  
‚Ä¢ Clay-coloured stools  
‚Ä¢ Abdominal discomfort  
‚Ä¢ Fever  
As with HIV (human immunodeficiency virus) and AIDS, signs and 
symptoms for Hepatitis B are unreliable and  may or may not be present. 
This is why proper testing for both  parties  is the only way to know if an 
infection is present.  
 
 
 
 

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -10 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  HBV Vaccine  
Risk may be mitigated with the Hepatitis B vaccine. Nurses should be offered 
Hepatitis B vaccinations and medical surveillance if they are exposed to 
hazardous substances.  
There is an effective vaccine for Hepatitis B  that is administered in three 
doses over a  six-month period. The vaccine is made from non -
infectious  materials and cannot cause one to become  infected with the 
Hepatitis B virus. Severe  problems or allergic rea ctions are rare. It is 80 ‚Äì
95% effective in providing  protection against the virus, but only in  situations 
where all three doses of the  vaccine are administered.  
Although the employer must offer the vaccine  to Nurses , Nurses  do not have 
to accept that offer.  Nurses  may opt to decline the vaccination series; in 
which case they will be  asked to sign a declination form. Even if the initial 
offer is  declined, the series can be chosen at  any time  during employment. 
For example, if one is exposed on the job at a later date, the vaccine can be 
chosen.  
Those who should consider getting the HBV  vaccine are:  
‚Ä¢ Professionals who provide medical care  
‚Ä¢ People who work in medical/or dental offices  
‚Ä¢ People who handle medical waste  
‚Ä¢ Employees who perform custodial duties that involve the cleaning of 
decontaminated  surfaces (blood and OPIM)  
‚Ä¢ People who administer first aid routinely  
‚Ä¢ Tattoo artists or anyone who performs body piercings or body art  
Hepatitis C  
The Hepatitis C virus (HCV) reproduces in the liver, which 
causes  inflammation that leads to cirrhosis of the liver, liver  cancer, and liver 
failure.  Some individuals exposed to Hepatitis C may possess the ability to 
combat the infection and successfully e liminate it from their system , while 
others may become  chronically infected. Approximately 80% of those 
exposed develop a chronic infection; 20% will clear the virus from their 
systems and develop a  natural immunity to it.  
 
 

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -11 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  Statistics within the Canada : 
‚Ä¢ It is estimated that 3.2 million people in the Canada  have a  chronic 
Hepatitis C infection.  
‚Ä¢ 17,000 people each year become infected with the  Hepatitis C virus.  
‚Ä¢ 12,000 people each year die from liver complications  caused by 
Hepatitis C.  
How is HCV Contracted?  
Hepatitis C is spread a little differently compared to Hepatitis B and  HIV. 
While the latter two viruses are mainly spread through sexual contact with 
an infected person, Hepatitis C is spread mostly through blood, including 
sharing needles  with an infecte d person (mostly due to injected drug use), 
and through  getting tattoos and piercings with unsterilized equipment.  
Also, like both HIV and Hepatitis B, the infection can be passed from  a 
mother to an unborn (or just -born) baby. In healthcare settings, it can 
also be passed on through needlestick injuries. It is worth noting that 
these  are both rare  cases , as is spreading the disease through sexual 
intercourse.  
HCV Signs and Symptoms  
Much like with HIV and Hepatitis B, signs and symptoms of Hepatitis C are 
unreliable  and may or may not be present. This is why proper testing for all 
of the above is the  only way to confidently know if an infection is present.  
Hepatitis C symptoms (which mirror those of Hepatitis B) include, but are 
not limited to:  
‚Ä¢ Yellow skin (jaundice)  
‚Ä¢ Yellowing eyes  
‚Ä¢ Tiredness and fatigue  
‚Ä¢ Loss of appetite  
‚Ä¢ Nausea  
‚Ä¢ Dark urine  
‚Ä¢ Joint pain  
‚Ä¢ Clay-coloured stools  
‚Ä¢ Abdominal discomfort  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -12 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  ‚Ä¢ Fever  
HCV Treatment  
Unfortunately, there is neither a Hepatitis C vaccine nor a known 
cure. However, there are new drugs that have come on the market for 
those  in need of Hepatitis C treatment.  
HIV (AIDS)  
HIV attacks  the body and harms the immune system by destroying the  white 
blood cells that fight infection, which in turn  diminishes the body's ability to 
protect itself. HIV eventually progresses  into AIDS ‚Äìacquired 
immunodeficiency syndrome. The HIV  virus is very fragile and  will 
not survive very long outside of  the human body.  
On average, it takes 10 years for the HIV virus to progress into  AIDS. 
However, this average varies greatly from person to  person and is affected 
by a number of factors such as health  status, behavioral characteristics, 
medications taken, etc.  
How is HIV Contracted?  
Only less than  one percent of people are infected while providing  medical 
care, and most of these are due to sticks from dirty needles. HIV 
transmission mostly happens through  unprotected sex and from sharing 
needles or syringes.  
HIV Signs and Symptoms  
The HIV virus is quite fragile (outside the body) and will die within  seconds 
after being exposed to air. Inside the body, the amount of the  virus present 
in bodily fluids and the physiological condition of the host  will determine how 
long the virus lives.  
When present, symptoms can include:  
‚Ä¢ Fever  
‚Ä¢ Fatigue  
‚Ä¢ Night sweats  
‚Ä¢ Weight loss  
‚Ä¢ Rash 
‚Ä¢ Dry cough  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -13 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  Skin Diseases  
The largest organ of the body is the skin.  Skin contains blood vessels, 
sensory receptors, nerves, and sweat glands. The thickness of skin varies 
from person to person, from around 1.5 millimeters to 4 millimeters.  
The layers of the skin include:  
‚Ä¢ The epidermis is the thick outer layer that we most likely associate 
with the skin.  
‚Ä¢ The dermis is the flexible second layer of skin. It is composed  mostly 
of connective tissue and filled with blood vessels and nerves.  
‚Ä¢ The hypodermis, also known as the  subcutaneous layer, is the 
innermost layer and is composed of fatty material.  
Unbroken Skin as Barrier  
Unbroken skin forms an impervious barrier  against bloodborne pathogens. 
However,  infected blood can enter a person 's system  through:  
‚Ä¢ Open sores  
‚Ä¢ Cuts 
‚Ä¢ Abrasions  
‚Ä¢ Acne 
‚Ä¢ Any sort of damaged or broken skin,  such as sunburn or blisters  
Bloodborne pathogens may  also be  transmitted through  the mucous 
membranes of  the: 
‚Ä¢ Eyes 
‚Ä¢ Nose 
‚Ä¢ Mouth  
For example, a splash of  contaminated blood  to your  eyes, nose, or mouth 
could  result in  transmission.  
 
 
 
 

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -14 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  Skin Diseases  
Skin diseases and disorders include boils, open sores,  infected wounds, 
abrasions, weeping dermatological  lesions, and more.  It is important that 
anyone with  these sorts of conditions abstains from working if there  is any 
chance that they can contaminate h ealthcare  supplies, work surfaces, body 
art equipment, etc.  
Ideally, employees ' skin should be free of rashes and  infection, particularly 
for healthcare providers,  caregivers, tattoo artists, and the like. 
Alternatively,  you can also cover all open sores and wounds with  bandages 
to avoid any potential spread of disease if  the conditi on is not  too severe or 
contagious.  
Commonly Spread Skin Diseases  
Bacteria:  
‚Ä¢ Staphylococcus aureus is a germ that 30% of people have on their 
skin and in their noses. Staph infections look like pimples or boils. 
Most of the time, staph infections are easily treatable.  
‚Ä¢ Methicillin -resistant Staphylococcus aureus (MRSA) is a bacterium that 
causes infections in different parts of the body. It is more difficult to 
treat than most strains of staph, as it is resistant to some commonly 
used antibiotics.  
Viruses:  
‚Ä¢ The herpes simplex virus is a commonly spread skin infection that 
causes herpes. Herpes can appear in various parts of the body, most 
commonly on the face, scalp, arms, neck, and upper chest.  
‚Ä¢ Herpes is usually indicated by small round blisters. When broken, 
these blisters can secrete a clear or yellowish fluid. Contraction of 
herpes occurs from contact with infected saliva, mucous membranes, 
and skin.  
 
 
 
 
 

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -15 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  Fungus:  
‚Ä¢ Commonly spread fungus -related skin disorders include athlete's foot 
and ringworm. The only real difference between the two is location, as 
ringworm can develop on the skin, hair, nails, and scalp, whereas 
athlete's foot only occurs on the feet, mostly between the toes.  
‚Ä¢ The two both present similar signs of red, patchy, flaky, itchy skin. 
They are both also highly contagious and easily spread from one 
person to another, or through infected surfaces in warm, moist 
environments, like shower floors. Keeping areas susceptible  to 
athlete's foot clean and dry will go a long way to preventing the spread 
of the fungus.  
Universal Precautions  
It is important that you follow all practices that are designed to protect both 
yourself, the healthcare professional, and the clients you serve , as infections 
can easily spread from client to healthcare provider and then  onward to 
other clients.  
As a healthcare professional, part of your job is to protect against the spread 
of bloodborne pathogens and  infectious diseases. Furthermore, healthcare 
providers have an ethical and professional responsibility to adhere  to 
scientifically accepted or evide nce-based practices and principles of infection 
control, and to monitor the  performance of those for whom the healthcare 
provider is also responsible.  
Universal Precautions are a set of precautions designed to 
prevent  transmission of HIV, the Hepatitis B virus (HBV), and other 
bloodborne  pathogens when providing care. These precautions consider the 
blood and OPIM of all clients  potentially infectious. These are OHS-required 
practices that  require you to treat all blood and OPIM as if  it is known to be 
infectious.  
Universal Precautions include:  
‚Ä¢ Handwashing and hygiene  
‚Ä¢ Use of PPE (e.g., gloves, gowns, masks, eyewear)  
‚Ä¢ Respiratory hygiene and cough etiquette  
‚Ä¢ Sharps safety (engineering and work practice controls)  
o Safe injection practices  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -16 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  o Safe handling of potentially contaminated equipment or surfaces 
in a healthcare setting  
Handwashing  
Handwashing is the single most  effective way to prevent infection . Practice 
the following:  
‚Ä¢ Wash your hands before and  after contact is made with  clients.  
‚Ä¢ Wash your hands as often as  needed, as they become visibly  soiled , or 
when exposed to  infectious materials.  
‚Ä¢ Wash your hands using soap  and hot water immediately 
after removing your gloves.  
Wearing gloves is not a reason to  forego handwashing. Wearing gloves will 
not eliminate the necessity for  handwashing, which is the single most 
effective  way to prevent infection.  
Proper Handwashing Procedure  
How to wash your hands:  
1. Use a disposable paper towel to turn on the sink faucet.  
2. Thoroughly wet your hands.  
3. Apply a generous amount of soap.  
4. Rub hands palm to palm. Wet your hands with clean, preferably 
running water. Apply enough soap to cover all surfaces of your hands 
and wrists.  
5. Rub palm to palm with fingers interlaced. Lather and rub your hands 
together briskly and thoroughly. Make sure to scrub all surfaces of 
your hands, fingertips, fingernails, and wrists.  
6. Rub palm to palm with fingers interlaced with the palm of the other 
hand.  
7. Rub with the back of fingers to the opposing palm with fingers 
interlocked.  
8. Sweep hand from fingers to thumb.  
9. Rub tips fingers in opposite palms in a circular motion.  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -17 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  10. Rub each wrist with the opposite hand. Scrub your hands and wrists 
for at least 20 seconds.  
11. Rinse your hands and wrists under clean, preferably running water.  
12. Use a towel to turn off the faucet.  
13. Dry your hands with a disposable paper towel.  
14. See the training video, 7 Steps to Washing Your Hands Properly , 
found on SharePoint.  
If soap and water are not available, use an alcohol -based hand sanitizer that 
contains at least 60% alcohol. Alcohol -based hand sanitizers will quickly 
reduce the number of microbes on your hands, but they will not eliminate all 
types of germs.  
Proper Use of Hand Sanitizer  
Hand sanitizers that are alcohol -based are great options if soap and 
water  are not  available. Work the sanitizer into the skin as thoroughly 
as handwashing with soap and water.  
1. Fill the palm of one hand with hand sanitizer. Apply a very generous 
layer to both hands.  
2. Spread the hand sanitizer to cover all areas around the wrists, palms, 
on top of the hands, and between fingers . Work it into every crevice or 
wrinkle,  including cuticles, nail beds, and under rings.   
3. Continue to massage the hand sanitizer into your hands for 20 
seconds.  
Personal Protective Equipment  
PPE includes things like gloves,  gowns, and masks and should be  used or 
worn whenever exposure to  body fluids is anticipated.  
Rules to follow:  
‚Ä¢ Always wear PPE in  exposure situations.  
‚Ä¢ Remove PPE that is torn , punctured , or has lost its  ability to function 
as a barrier to bloodborne  pathogens.  
‚Ä¢ Remove PPE before leaving the work area.  
If you work in an area with routine exposure to blood  or potentially 
infectious materials, the necessary PPE  should be readily accessible. 

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -18 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  Contaminated gloves,  clothing, PPE, or other materials should be placed  in 
appropriately labeled bags or containers until  disposed of, decontaminated, 
or laundered. It is  important to find out where these bags or containers  are 
located in your area before beginning your work.  
Gloves  
Gloves provide a protective barrier. Gloves should be made of latex, nitrile, 
rubber, or other water -impervious materials. If glove material is thin or 
flimsy, double gloving can provide an additional layer of protection. Also, if 
you know you have cuts or  sores on your hands, you should cover these 
with a bandage or similar protection as an additional precaution before 
donning your gloves.  
Glove Removal and Putting Gloves On  
When putting on gloves, size matters. Gloves come in many sizes. One size 
rarely  fits all. Properly fitting gloves will enhance the Nurse's tactile 
sensitivity, allowing for more precise actions during delicate tasks like IV 
insertion, and minimizing the risk of tearing and contamination. A proper fit 
also reduces hand fatigue, which can result from wearing gloves that are too 
small and constrictive, or too large and cumbersome.  
Inspect the gloves for any imperfections such as punctures, tears, or cuts. If 
a glove is damaged, do not use it! It is imperative that gloves remain intact; 
any breach compromises the necessary protective barrier essential for 
maintaining safety. A damage d glove could result in the transmission of 
infectious agents instead of their containment and eradication. Prioritize 
safety at all times.  
To correctly put on surgical gloves, a Nurse should:  
1. Start with clean, dry hands and open the glove package on a sterile 
surface.  
2. With one hand, pick up a glove by the folded cuff edge, being careful 
not to touch the outside of the glove.  
3. Slip your fingers into the glove and pull toward your wrist, adjusting for 
comfort and fit.  
4. To don the second glove, slide your gloved fingers under the cuff of the 
remaining glove and repeat the process without touching the exterior.  
5. Ensure that there is no skin exposure between the gloves and the 
gown sleeves.  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -19 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  Gloves will sometimes stick together , though most gloves now have a 
coating  or powder on them to prevent this. Do not blow a puff of air into the 
glove. This  is not  appropriate; you do not  want to spread any germs  you 
may have onto the clean gloves.  
When taking contaminated gloves off, do so carefully. Make sure you do not 
touch the outside of the gloves with any bare skin  and be sure to dispose of 
them in a proper container so that no one will come in contact with them.  
To properly remove gloves, follow these steps:  
1. Pinch the palm side of one glove on the outside near your wrist. 
(Glove -on-glove contact only.)  
2. Pull the glove slowly and carefully toward your fingertips, turning it 
inside out as you pull it off your hand.  
3. Wad up the dirty glove into the palm of your still -gloved hand.  
4. Carefully slip two fingers under the wrist of the other glove. Avoid 
touching the outside of the glove. (Skin -on-skin contact only.)  
5. Pull the glove slowly and carefully toward your fingertips, turning it 
inside out as you pull it off your hand. The other glove is now 
contained inside.  
6. Toss both gloves into the trash along with other PPE. Ideally, you 
will have access to a trash receptacle that you can open using a foot 
pedal. Make sure the liner is appropriate for handling bloodborne 
pathogens and OPIM per your regulations.  
7. Finally, wash your hands thoroughly with soap and running  water, if 
available. Otherwise, rub your hands thoroughly with an alcohol -based 
hand sanitizer if they are not visibly soiled, and then wash your hands 
as soon as it is practical.  
When removing your gloves, it is important that you do not snap the glove 
material, so make sure you have a good grip and work slowly and carefully. 
Snapping the glove's materials could send pathogens and infectious 
materials flying into eyes and other muc ous membranes or onto clean 
surfaces.  
 

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -20 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  Goggles  
Anytime there is a risk of splashing or vaporization of contaminated fluids, 
goggles and/or other eye protection should be used to protect your eyes. 
Again, bloodborne pathogens can be transmitted through the thin 
membranes of the eyes, so it is important to protect them. Splashing could 
occur while cleaning up a spill, during laboratory procedures, or while 
providing first aid or medical assistance.  
Face Shields  
Face shields may be worn in addition to goggles to provide additional face 
protection. A face shield will protect against splashes to the nose and mouth.  
Aprons  
Aprons may be worn to protect your clothing and to keep blood or other 
contaminated fluids from soaking through to your skin.  
Respiratory Hygiene/Cough Etiquette  
Implement measures to prevent the spread of respiratory infections from 
anyone in a healthcare setting with signs or symptoms. Post signs at 
entrances asking Team Members and clients with symptoms of respiratory 
infection to:  
‚Ä¢ Cover their mouth and nose when coughing or sneezing.  
‚Ä¢ Use tissues and throw them away.  
‚Ä¢ Wash their hands or use a hand sanitizer every time they touch their 
mouth or nose.  
Provide tissues and no -touch receptacles for their disposal. Provide 
resources for performing hand hygiene in or near waiting areas.  
Offer masks to symptomatic clients  when they enter the lounge area. 
Provide space and encourage symptomatic clients  to sit as far away from 
others as possible. Locations may wish to place these clients  in a separate 
area, if available.  
 
 

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -21 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  Engineering Control and Work Practice  
Engineering controls and work practice controls will be used to prevent or 
minimize exposure to bloodborne pathogens.  
The fundamental method of protecting yourself against pathogens and 
infection is controlling the hazards. This can be accomplished in many ways, 
including:  
‚Ä¢ Elimination:  Get rid/remove all hazards or hazardous tasks, if possible.  
‚Ä¢ Substitution:  Replace hazards or hazardous tasks with safer 
equipment and/or safer methods.  
‚Ä¢ Engineering Controls:  Use devices such as self -sheathing needles and 
sharps containers to block or remove your risks of getting stuck, 
poked, or cut.  
‚Ä¢ PPE: Know the location of your PPE and how to use it properly. Also, 
keep in mind that PPE only protects you if you use it.  
‚Ä¢ Work Practice and Administrative Controls:  Follow the policies and 
procedures for your workplace to eliminate all risks associated with 
bloodborne pathogens and OPIM.  
Workplace administrative and engineering controls include:  
‚Ä¢ Food, drink, etc.:  Never eat, drink, smoke, apply cosmetics, or handle 
contact lenses in any work areas where there exists the possibility of 
exposure to bloodborne pathogens and OPIM.  
‚Ä¢ Trash disposal:  When disposing of any trash that contains 
contaminated materials, do not compress the trash with your hand. 
Also, when carrying contaminated materials for disposal, be sure to 
carry the trash away from your body in case of spillage.  
‚Ä¢ Environment and work surfaces:  Thoroughly clean and decontaminate 
all equipment and surfaces in your work environment after all contact 
with blood, other body fluids, and OPIM.  
 
 
 
 

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -22 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  ‚Ä¢ Contaminated sharp objects:  When dealing with contaminated needles 
and other sharp objects (routinely shortened to just "sharps") , follow 
certain guidelines, such as not using bent needles, recapping needles, 
and trying to remove questionable needles. All needles and sharps 
must also be placed into appropriate sharps containers immediately 
after use. The DRIPBaR does not recommend the recapping of used 
needles. This is to reduce the likelihood of exposure. We also strongly 
recommend the use of safety needle s. 
‚Ä¢ Warning labels:  Warning labels should be affixed to all regulated 
waste and other containers that are used to store, transport, or ship 
other potentially infected materials. Labels must be fluorescent orange 
or red, or at least predominantly orange or red to indicate a p ossible 
threat, along with lettering and symbols in a contrasting colo ur. 
‚Ä¢ PPE: All employees must be provided with personal protective 
equipment by their employer at no cost to the employee.  
‚Ä¢ Examples of PPE include:  
o Gloves:  Wear gloves whenever the potential exists for touching 
blood, bodily fluids, or other potentially contaminated items.  
o CPR Shields and Protective Eyewear:  Use these items when 
there is a likelihood of blood and OPIM being secreted or 
splashed.  
o Gowns:  Wear a gown when the potential exists of getting blood 
and other bodily fluids on any clothing or exposed area.  
o Masks and Respirators:  Use whenever there is a potential risk of 
coming into contact with airborne infectious diseases.  
The DRIPBaR Sharps Disposal Policy  
The best way to limit the likelihood of bloodborne pathogen transmission is 
to reduce the chances of sharps injuries. You must contract with a  licensed 
biomedical waste disposal service  approved in your province and follow all 
local laws on storing, labeling, and discarding sharps/medical waste. Proper 
sharps waste disposal is a key strategy for curbing these events:  
‚Ä¢ Sharps (needles) should be placed in specially designed sharps 
containers after use that have HC-marked clearance. These 
receptacles are made of hard plastic and are designed to prevent 
punctures and leaks. They are also clearly labeled as biohazardous to 
ensure proper handling.

sharps are used, including exam rooms and laboratories. They should 
be positioned as close as possible to the point of use and at a height 
that is easily accessible.  
‚Ä¢ Sharps containers are reserved for items that are contaminated and

‚Ä¢ Nurses, doctors, and other sharps users are responsible for proper

Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  Handling Contaminated Laundry  
If you are tasked with laundering contaminated items like  reusable gowns 
rather than disposing of them, make sure  you follow your facility's specific 
procedures for cleaning  and handling these items.  
General laundry procedures will include:  
‚Ä¢ Wearing PPE whenever handling contaminated laundry.  
‚Ä¢ Keeping contaminated laundry separate from non -contaminated 
laundry.  
‚Ä¢ Bagging potentially contaminated laundry separate  from non -
contaminated laundry.  
‚Ä¢ Using leakproof bags for wet contaminated laundry.  
‚Ä¢ Transporting contaminated laundry in properly labeled  bags, especially 
when shipping it to an offsite facility.  
Regulated Waste  
What is regulated waste?  
The OHS Bloodborne Pathogens Standard defines regulated  waste as:  
‚Ä¢ Any liquid or semi -liquid blood or other potentially  infectious material  
‚Ä¢ Contaminated items that would release blood or OPIM in a  liquid or 
semi-liquid state if compressed or wrung out  
‚Ä¢ Items caked with dried blood or OPIM that are  capable of flaking off 
and releasing these materials during  handling  
‚Ä¢ Contaminated sharps  
‚Ä¢ Pathological and microbiological wastes containing blood or  OPIM  
It is important to note that all properly labeled and bundled  waste should be 
handled according to your facility's disposal  procedures. It is also important 
to consider any provincial  or local  requirements that may apply to regulated 
waste disposal in  your area.  
While this should go without saying, never dispose of  potentially 
contaminated waste into normal trash receptacles.  
 
 

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -26 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  Regulated Waste Containers  
All blood and other potentially infectious material must be disposed of 
in properly labeled biohazard containers, in either a red bag or 
a predominantly orange and red container that has been imprinted with 
the biohazard symbol.  
Regulated waste containers must be:  
1. Sealable: You must be able to close and seal the container completely.  
2. Properly Constructed: The container must be able to properly handle 
its contents without fail.  
3. Leakproof: The regulated waste container must prevent leakage of all 
fluids and  materials while handling, storing, transporting, and 
shipping.  
Sharps Containers  
All items falling into this category ‚Äî like needles, syringes, and razors ‚Äî 
must be placed into sealable, leakproof, puncture -resistant containers. 
The containers must also be properly labeled or colo ur-coded. Regardless of 
type, all regulated waste containers should be routinely  inspected and 
replaced, and they should never  be allowed to overfill.  
Signs Labels, and Colo ur-Coding  
Warning labels need to be affixed to regulated waste, refrigerators, and 
freezers  containing blood or other potentially infectious material; as well as 
other containers used to store, transport, or  ship blood or other potentially 
infectious material. These  labels are fluorescent orange, red, or orange -red, 
and they are available from Environmental Health and Safety (EHS.) Bags 
used to dispose of regulated waste must be bagged to guard  against the 
possibility of leakage if the first bag is punctured.  
Labels should display th e universal biohazard symbol.  
All regulated waste must be disposed of in properly labeled containers or red 
biohazard bags. These must  be disposed of at an approved facility. Most 
departments or facilities that generate regulated waste will  have some sort 
of contract with an outside di sposal company that will come to pick up their 
medical waste and take  it to an approved incineration/disposal facility.  
 

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -27 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  Non-regulated waste  that is not generated by a medical -type facility or 
human health -related research  laboratory may be disposed of in regulated 
plastic trash bags if it has been decontaminated or autoclaved  before 
disposal.  
All bags containing such materials must be labeled, signed, and dated, 
verifying that the materials inside have been  decontaminated according to 
acceptable procedures and post -health threats. Labels must be placed on 
the bags,  so they  are readily visible.  
Disposal of Needles and Glassware  
Needles:  
‚Ä¢ Needles should never be recapped. The DRIPBaR does not recommend 
the recapping of used needles. This is to reduce the likelihood of 
exposure. We also strongly recommend the use of safety needles.  
‚Ä¢ Needles should be moved only by using a mechanical device or tool 
such as forceps,  pliers, or broom and dustpan.  
‚Ä¢ Never break or shear needles.  
‚Ä¢ Needles shall be disposed of in labeled sharps containers only.  
‚Ä¢ Sharps containers shall be closable, puncture -resistant, leakproof on 
sides and bottom,  and must be labeled or colo ur-coded.  
‚Ä¢ When sharps containers are being moved from the  area of use, the 
containers should be closed immediately before removal or 
replacement to  prevent spillage or protrusion of contents during 
handling or transport.  
Broken glassware:  
‚Ä¢ Broken glassware that has been visibly contaminated with blood must 
be sterilized with  an approved disinfectant solution before it is 
disturbed or cleaned up.  
o Glassware that has been decontaminated may be disposed of in 
an appropriate  sharps  container (i.e., closable, puncture -
resistant, leakproof on sides and bottom, with  appropriate 
labels).  
‚Ä¢ Broken glassware should never be picked up directly with hands. 
Sweep or brush the  material into a dustpan.  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -28 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  o Uncontaminated broken glassware may be disposed of in a 
closable, puncture -resistant  container such as a cardboard box 
or coffee can.  
By using Universal Precautions and following these simple engineering and 
work practice  controls, you can protect against and prevent transmission of 
bloodborne pathogens.  
Bodily Fluid Cleanup Procedure  
To clean up bodily fluid, t here are two essential supplies you need: paper 
towels and bleach.  Do not  use toilet paper, napkins, or even  low-
quality  paper towels.  The paper towels you use should be commercial grade 
and able to  withstand the task at hand without falling apart.  
Bleach is super cheap and super effective, so there is no point 
in substituting. However, if  you are  going to substitute, make sure 
the cleaner or disinfectant  you are  using is up to the job and is 
specifically  manufactured to kill microorganisms and prote ct against all 
viruses,  bacteria, and other pathogens and infectious materials.  
The Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC) recommends a  bleach 
solution of  one part bleach to nine parts water.  This solution  should be 
strong enough to kill any bloodborne pathogens and  infectious materials you 
may encounter.  
In general, when handling or cleaning up infectious materials and 
bloodborne pathogens, your goal is to create barriers. These barriers will 
halt the spread of infection, whether the barrier is a piece of protective 
clothing or a safe container to dispose of infectious materials.  
1. Put on your first pair of medical -grade gloves and remove any sharp 
objects.  
2. Wipe up as much  of the dried blood as you can using a paper towel. Be 
sure to keep the blood isolated  on the table or the paper  towels. If 
blood is on the floor, be sure to disinfect the floor as well.  
3. Remove your first pair of gloves using a glove on the glove for the first, 
and a bare  finger against your wrist for the  second. Throw  away both 
gloves.  
4. Put on a second pair of medical -grade gloves.  
5. Mix your bleach solution in a spray bottle and liberally spray the 
solution on the table where the dried  blood was located.  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -29 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  6. Wipe down with paper towels and make sure all the blood is visibly 
gone.  
7. Remove your second pair of gloves using the same protective method 
as before.  
8. Put on your third pair of gloves. (This may seem inconvenient, but it is 
necessary to ensure thorough cleanliness and reduce the risk of 
infection due to dirty gloves.)  
9. Lightly mist the surface of the table with your bleach solution and allow 
it to evaporate. The time it takes for the liquid solution to evaporate is 
equal to the time it takes to kill any remaining pathogens completely.  
Safe Injection Practices  
Safer Devices Means Safer Environment  
For employers, the Needlestick Safety and Prevention Act requires that 
they provide appropriate, effective, safe, and commercially available  medical 
devices that are designed to eliminate or minimize  occupational exposure if 
unsafe injection practices ever occur.  
Safe injection practices are instrumental when  following the 
standard  precautions and include safe sharps and disposal practices ‚Äî such 
as using leakproof, puncture -resistant, appropriate sharps containers 
‚Äî along with using sharps and needle devices that have been 
engineered  with injection protection s. This includes any time you withdraw 
blood  or other bodily fluids, access a client 's vein or artery, or 
administer  medications and other fluids.  
The most common unsafe injection practices include reusing needles,  using 
multiple -dose medication or bags of solutions, and  administering the same 
intravenous medications to multiple clients . 
Do not  bend, break, or recap needles. A Nurse should never recap a needle 
after it has been administered to a client.  
Ensure your practices align with Public Health Agency of Canada guidelines 
and any  provincial public health requirements  on safe injection protocols.  
 
 
 

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -30 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  Aseptic Technique Means Safer Environment  
Make sure to always use aseptic techniques to avoid the contamination of 
sterile injection  equipment. This includes washing your hands, using clean 
gloves, and using alcohol wipes  to clean injection portals, the tops of vials, 
and the skin where needles wi ll be inserted.  
Remember to always change your gloves between clients  and between tasks 
that may  increase the chances of infection.  It is a good idea to have a 
generous supply of gloves,  so you  do not  run out.  
Never administer medication from a single syringe to multiple clients , even if 
the needle  has been replaced. Needles and syringes are sterile, single -use 
items. You can remember  this rule with this little ditty: "One needle, one 
syringe, one time only!"  
Other single -use pieces of equipment are fluid infusion and administration 
sets (IVs, IV  bags, tubing, connectors, etc.) and syringe and needle 
cannulas. These are single -use items. Dispose of these items safely. 
Remember, if any sterile item touches a non -sterile item,  throw it away and 
get another.  
When preparing an injection, keep work areas free of clutter and wash your 
hands  appropriately. Gloves should always be worn with any injection 
involving a client.  
If using a glass ampoule,  do not  use your bare fingers to open it. Instead, 
use a gauze pad to  break the seal and use a filtered needle before 
administering the medication to the  client.  
Also, make sure to check the name, dosage, and proper delivery method 
before  administering any medication. Never  re-enter the same vial with the 
same needle and  same syringe.  
Always  use a new needle and syringe.  
Single -Dose Versus Multi -Dose Vials  
Use single -dose vials whenever possible to reduce the chances of  infection, 
especially when the same medication is being administered  to multiple 
clients. Do not  combine the contents of one vial with  another.  
If you are  using multi -dose vials, make sure both the syringe and the  needle 
are sterile. If there is any doubt, toss them out. Store all  medication vials 
according to the manufacturer's recommendations  and do not  keep multi -
dose vials in client treatment areas.  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -31 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  Also, do not  use bags or bottles of IV solutions as a common source 
of supply for multiple clients.  
Exposure Incident and Reporting  
Additional OHS Requirements  
Incidents must be reported to the provincial Workers ' Compensation Board  
(WCB) within the timeframe mandated in your province. You must maintain 
incident logs per local OHS rules.  
There are OHS requirements related to exposure:  
‚Ä¢ OHS requires that any ECP reflect how employers  implement  new 
developments in control technology.  
‚Ä¢ OHS requires employers to solicit input from employees who 
are responsible for  direct client  care, and this includes the 
identification,  evaluation, and selection  of better engineering and work 
practice controls.  
‚Ä¢ OHS also requires that certain employers establish and maintain a 
Sharps Injury Log  of all percutaneous injuries from contaminated 
sharps. To properly evaluate future risks and device effectiveness, the 
Sharps Injury Log  must include, at least, the incident 's location, the 
device involved, and a description of the incident.  
‚Ä¢ Employers must record all work -related sharps injuries involving 
contaminated objects on the OHS 300 and OHS 301 logs.  
The DRIPBaR Needlestick Injury Protocol  
The DRIPBaR does not recommend the recapping of used needles. This is in 
order to reduce the likelihood of exposure. We also strongly recommend the 
use of safety needles.  
Follow these steps:  
1. First, after an accidental needlestick injury with potential exposure to 
bloodborne pathogens, employees should safely dispose of the needle 
and wash the site with soap and warm water immediately!  
2. The employee should then notify the Lead Nurse, Owner, and Medical 
Director.  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -32 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  3. It is recommended that the employee seek medical attention at an 
urgent care or emergency room as soon as possible for baseline labs 
after the needlestick injury.  
4. If the employee requests to seek immediate care, arrangements should 
be made to cover their shift.  
5. Fill out an Incident Report with detailed information and email to 
Medical@ TheDRIPB aR.com and the Franchise Owner.  
6. Each location has its own worker 's compensation insurance coverage. 
Employees should seek care at partnering facilities that are defined in 
policy guidelines.  
7. The location should establish a quick reference guide , including the 
carrier 's website and the policy number.  
8. The employee then proceeds to fill out a claim form.  
9. The employee is to follow the recommendations for wound care and lab 
frequency as outlined by an urgent care or emergency room provider.  
10. The employee will provide updates to the Franchise Owner and the 
Medical Team, as necessary.  
Exposure Incident  
If you are ever stuck by another sharp or get blood or OPIM in 
your eyes,  nose, mouth, or by contact with broken skin, immediately flood 
the exposed area  with water.  
Clean any wound or broken skin with soap and  water or a skin  disinfectant, 
if available. Report the incident to your  employer and get immediate  medical 
attention.  
If you are  exposed, take the following steps immediately:  
1. Clean the contaminated area thoroughly with soap and water. 
Wash  cuts and exposed skin with soap and water.  
2. Flush out any splashes of blood and OPIM to the mouth and nose 
with water.  
3. If blood is splashed in the mucous membrane, flush the affected  area 
with running water for at least 15 minutes.  
4. If the eyes are involved, irrigate with clean water, saline, or 
sterile  irritants  for 20 minutes.  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -33 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  5. Seek immediate follow -up care as identified in the location 's ECP.  
6. You will  also need to report the incident to the Franchise Owner and 
Medical Team and complete all the appropriate  Incident Report forms 
as soon as possible after the exposure incident. However,  do not  delay 
medical treatment to fill out paperwork.  
Exposure Incident Reporting  
An exposure incident should include the following:  
1. The time, date, and location of the exposure.  
2. An account of all the people involved, including the exposed 
person,  names of their first aid providers, and if possible, the name of 
the source  individual.  
3. The circumstances of the exposure, any actions taken after the 
exposure,  and any other information required by your employer.  
Report the exposure incident to the appropriate person identified in 
your employer's ECP (often the infection control officer).  There will be forms 
to fill out and continued follow -up, which will proceed  according to your 
employer's policies.  
Your employer's ECP must specify who should be  contacted and what 
procedures need to be done to follow  up. This  includes the employer's 
responsibilities to provide post -exposure  prophylaxis when medically 
indicated, counseling, and the evaluation of  reported illnesses at no charge.

Training  
Employers must ensure that their workers receive  regular training that 
covers all elements of the  standard including, but not 
limited  to, information  on bloodborne pathogens and diseases, methods  used 
to control occupational exposure, Hepatitis B  vaccine, and medical 
evaluation and post -exposure  follow -up procedures.  
Employers must offer this training on initial  assignment, at least annually 
thereafter, and when  new or modified tasks or procedures affect a  worker 's 
occupational exposure. Also, HIV and HBV  laboratory and production facility 
workers must  receive specialized Initial Training, in addition to the  training 
provided to all workers with occupational  exposure. Workers must have the 
opportunity to ask  the tr ainer questions. Training must be  presented at an 
educational level and in a language  that the workers unde rstand.  
Medical  
Medical recordkeeping requirements:  
‚Ä¢ Employee 's name and Social Insurance  number  
‚Ä¢ Employee 's Hepatitis B vaccination  status  
‚Ä¢ Results of examinations, medical testing, post -exposure evaluation, 
and follow -up procedures  
‚Ä¢ Health care professional 's written  opinion  
‚Ä¢ Information provided to the health  care professional  
OHS Standards Beyond Bloodborne Pathogens  
1. Hazard Communication Standard:  This standard gives guidance on 
how an employer should properly  communicate about workplace 
hazards to his or her staff. Hazards  include anything from structural 
dangers to chemicals and exposure  to contaminated equipment/  
material. Like the bloodborne  pathogen 's standard, this must be 
communicated to all employees working in a  medical office, including a 
third-party cleaning staff.  While the same types of records (training 
records, hazard assessments, incident logs) are kept, 
each provincial  OHS code may impose unique forms or reporting 
structures.  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -38 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  2. Ionizing Radiation Standard:  Guidelines from this standard refer to 
protocol around x -ray and  diagnostic imaging equipment. Under the 
standard, employees must  be informed when such equipment is 
present, what the equipment is,  where restricted areas exist, and how 
to stay safe if an e mployee  must enter one of those restricted areas. 
These guidelines also  include requirements for labeling machinery and 
equipment.  
3. Exit Routes Standard:  You have probably seen part of this one if you 
have ever been in a medical  office. The exit routes standard provides 
guidance on how to inform  employees of the protocol for emergency 
exits. Medical facilities must  have exit routes large enough to 
accommod ate the number of people  inside the building, and there 
must be diagrams of evacuation routes  posted in plain sight.  
4. Electrical Standards:  Requirements for electrical standards include 
electrical equipment and  wiring, especially in hazardous locations. If 
the medical facility uses  any flammable gases, these guidelines outline 
special wiring or  equipment you may need to install. This one is t ricky 
for medical  office cleaners because there is not much you can do to 
enforce the  standards since the medical office controls them. However, 
it is a good  idea to educate employees in these standards so they can 
inform you  and the office if they come ac ross any violations.  
5. OHS Poster:  This is another OHS checklist for a medical office that you 
might be  familiar with. The OHS poster (or provincial  plan equivalent) 
outlines  your rights and how to report any violations. The poster must 
be in plain sight, like in a break room or on a community bulletin 
board, so  this one should be easy to spot.  
6. Stay Updated:  It is the job of both the employer and the employee to 
stay updated on OHS regulations, as these do change periodically. 
Employers must provide the updates, though failure to do so does not 
necessarily exclude an employee from having to know the law.  
7. Random Inspections:  Finally, be ready for random inspections. OHS is 
allowed to show up at  any facility unannounced, which can create 
headaches and more if your  facility or employees are not in 
compliance. Medical offices are some of  the most frequent stops on 
these random visits, so it behooves your  team to check in at  least 
once a week to make sure they are in  compliance.  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -39 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  OHS WHMIS 2015 or Hazardous Products Act  Compliance  
OHS mandates that employers communicate with employees about 
hazardous chemicals they may be exposed to at work. The OHS Hazard 
Communication Standard, commonly referred to as "HazCom, " details what 
information needs to be shared, by when, and to whom. It is divided into 
five primary activities:  
1. Maintaining a master list of hazardous chemicals used within the 
facility.  
2. Creating a written hazard communication program.  
3. Properly labeling dangerous chemicals.  
4. Making Safety Data Sheets (SDS) readily accessible.  
5. Providing comprehensive staff training.  
In 2012, OHS aligned the Hazard Communication Standard with the  Globally 
Harmonized System  (GHS), a set of internationally created and approved 
criteria for classifying health, physical, and environmental hazards. The GHS 
specifies information that organizations must use to describe hazardous 
chemicals on any labels, SDS, and other communications.  
OHS has established both health and physical hazard classes for different 
chemicals. Health hazards reflect how the chemical affects a person's health. 
This may include:  
‚Ä¢ Toxicity  
‚Ä¢ Carcinogenicity  
‚Ä¢ Skin corrosion  
‚Ä¢ Eye damage  
‚Ä¢ Aspiration risk  
Physical hazards describe a chemical 's composition that could cause harm. 
For example, a chemical may be described as flammable, corrosive, an 
oxidizer, self -heating, etc.  
These hazard classes are also given a category designation to outline how 
dangerous the item is on a scale of 1 -4, with 1 being the most hazardous 
and 4 being the least hazardous.  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -40 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  If they handle controlled products, e mployees must receive WHMIS training, 
maintain Safety Data Sheets (SDS), and ensure labeling is GHS -compliant . 
OHS's Hazard Communication Standard Compliance 
Requirements  
The material for this topic came from the Stericycle.com.  
Written Program  
The Hazard Communication Standard requires healthcare organizations like 
The DRIPBaR to have a written program that describes what risks employees 
may be exposed to due to hazardous chemicals and how it will share 
information about those risks and any pote ntial health issues that could 
result with employees.  
Healthcare organizations must maintain a list of the hazardous substances 
they have on site. Conduct an audit in which a team walks through the 
location to identify hazardous chemicals and compiles a list. Franchise 
Owners or their designees may reach out to Team Members and request 
them to supply a list of hazardous chemicals found in their are as. 
The level of detail required in these programs is significant, and a franchised 
location may want to start with a generic plan created by an experienced 
third party and modify it as needed to reflect the location 's unique 
characteristics. By taking this approach, a location can be sure to include all 
required elements.  
Healthcare organizations should also review and update their programs 
regularly to reflect regulatory changes and make sure their written programs 
are readily accessible to staff.  
Hazardous Chemical Labeling  
According to GHS requirements, the manufacturer 's label on hazardous 
chemical containers must provide the following information:  
‚Ä¢ The product identifier or name  
‚Ä¢ A signal word that underscores the threat, such as "danger " or 
"warning " 
‚Ä¢ A hazard statement that relates to the relevant hazard class and 
category  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -41 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  ‚Ä¢ Pictograms  or symbols that convey health and physical information 
(These visual icons show hazard types. For instance, the skull and 
crossbones denote toxicity. Based on the GHS requirements, OHS 
adopted eight pictographs, including ones that represent flammability, 
corrosiveness, toxicity, and more. ) 
‚Ä¢ Precautionary statements that provide measures to minimize or 
prevent adverse effects  
‚Ä¢ Supplier information, including name, address, and telephone number  
While product labels are provided by the manufacturer or supplier, if 
chemicals are removed from the original, labeled container, organizations 
must create a workplace label that includes the above information and apply 
it to the secondary container.  
However, there are a few exceptions to this requirement. For example, an 
employee is not required to label a secondary container if they intend to use 
the chemical immediately. Also, when a pharmacy dispenses drugs to a 
healthcare provider for direct admin istration to a client , the healthcare 
provider is exempted from the secondary labeling requirement.  
In some cases, organizations can use alternatives to labels as long as they 
convey all the necessary information. Such alternatives should be discussed 
in the written hazard communication program, and organizations should 
provide training about the alterna tives to employees.  
Safety Data Sheets  
SDS communicates essential information about hazardous chemicals, 
including their properties and risks, correct handling, and storage practices, 
as well as what protective measures employees should use to remain safe 
when handling or interacting with chemi cals. 
The DRIPBaR franchised locations must have a Safety Data Sheet for each 
hazardous chemical in their inventories and ensure this information is readily 
accessible in the work area for Team Members who encounter the chemical.  
Training Requirements  
OHS requires organizations to provide hazard communication training at two 
different times. First, workers should receive training when they are initially 
assigned to a position where there is a potential for hazardous chemical 
exposure.  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -42 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  Second, training should be required whenever there is a new hazardous 
chemical introduced to their work environment for which they have not been 
previously trained.  
Training topics to cover include:  
‚Ä¢ The nature of the hazards present  
‚Ä¢ How employees can protect themselves, including the use of  PPE, 
workplace practices, and emergency procedures  
‚Ä¢ How to access the hazard communication program and master list of 
dangerous chemicals  
‚Ä¢ When it is necessary to create a label and proper labeling elements  
‚Ä¢ The purpose of material SDS and how to access them

specifically for that location. A detailed plan provides a framework for 
training the staff. It ensures that all Team Members are aware of their roles 
and responsibilities during an emergency, which leads to a more coordinated 
and effective response. Training based on the plan, coupled wit h regular 
drills, prepares Team Members to respond automatically and effectively 
when an actual emergency occurs. Document all training and drills. See the 
Emergency Drill Report on the next two pages.

Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  Medical Emergencies Policy and Procedures  
Policy  
It is the policy of The DRIPBaR that we will make every effort to ensure the 
workplace is safe and healthy for all employees. In this effort, we are 
establishing a protocol for action and whom to contact in the event of a 
medical emergency at the worksite.  
Procedures  
First Priority: Call 911  
If severe illness or injury is possibly a life -threatening medical situation, first 
call 911 for emergency services immediately. Examples of life -threatening 
situations include severe chest pains, uncontrolled bleeding, loss of 
consciousness, or violent be havior.  
Second Priority: Contact First Responders  
This is the protocol for contacting any first responders. First responders may 
include:  
‚Ä¢ Security personnel  
‚Ä¢ Supervisors  
‚Ä¢ Other employees who are First Aid/CPR trained  
Employers should post updated First Responder Lists with contact 
information throughout the location, especially near emergency phones. 
Identify if there is a protocol, such as paging a certain code name or calling 
one number, to reach first responders on site. 
First responders may provide basic First Aid or CPR until emergency medical 
services are able to take over.  
Third Priority: Station an Employee  
The DRIPBaR location will station an employee at the building entrance to 
direct emergency medical services (EMS) to the location of the medical 
emergency. The DRIPBaR employee will then attempt to contact the victim's 
designated emergency contact person.  
 

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual          Safety and Exposure Prevention  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page J -47 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  Ways to Assist in Medical Emergency  
‚Ä¢ Direct someone or clearly state you will call 911 as needed.  
‚Ä¢ Direct someone or clearly state you will contact/locate first responders.  
‚Ä¢ Identify and/or remove any dangers to the safety of the victim or 
others in the vicinity. Do not  move the victim unless their life is 
endangered by their current location.  
‚Ä¢ Attend to the victim as needed/able to until someone more qualified 
can take over (this may be just talking to them reassuringly or holding 
their hand). If they are conscious, ask for permission before giving 
care. 
‚Ä¢ Direct someone or clearly state you will contact/locate someone in HR.  
‚Ä¢ Take note of specifics (who was involved, what happened when it 
occurred, where it occurred). This information may be needed by EMS, 
first responders, and Human Resources/Franchise Owner.  
‚Ä¢ Double -check that someone is at the front to meet EMS.  
‚Ä¢ Reduce unnecessary employee traffic around the area.  
An Incident Report must be completed within 48 hours by the employee's 
supervisor or the supervisor of the area in which the incident occurred, if not 
an employee. Statements should be obtained from others involved and 
witnesses.  
Protecting Client Health  
Emergency Medications and Protocol  
First, in the event of an emergency, stop IV infusion !!! Assess the client's 
situation and have the Front Desk staff call 911. Use the automated external 
defibrillator (AED) if needed.  
Vitals should be continuously monitored!  
Use the emergency medicines found in the storage cabinet in a bin labeled 
"Emergency Medicines ": 
‚Ä¢ Epinephrine for anaphylaxis  
o Break the top of ampoule and using a filter needle, draw up 
epinephrine (1mg/ml) 0.3mL.

Regulatory Standards and Environmental 
Classifications for Sterile Compounding  
Sterile compounding operations are governed by two separate but 
complementary sets of standards. The first set, comprising USP 797 and 
National Association of Pharmacy Regulatory Authorities  (NAPRA ) guidelines, 
outlines the procedural requirements that Nurses must follow during the 
actual compounding process. The second set, consisting of ISO 5 class 100 
and ISO 7 class 10,000 specifications, defines the environmental and 
equipment standards necessar y to maintain sterile conditions in both the 
compounding h ood and the surrounding clean room area.  
The Canadian equivalent falls NAPRA maintains two key sets of standards: 
the Model Standards for Pharmacy Compounding of Non -hazardous Sterile 
Preparation and the Model Standards for Pharmacy Compounding of 
Hazardous Sterile Preparations. In Canada, the ph armaceutical compounding 
procedures for safe and compounding is under the jurisdiction of the 
National Association of Pharmacy Regulatory Authority (NAPRA). Since 
NAPRA's procedures closely align with USP 797, The DRIPBaR locations 
adhere to USP 797 standa rds, which are United States pharmaceutical 
compounding procedures . 
The physical environment for compounding is regulated by International 
Organization for Standardization (ISO) classifications, which are identical in 
both countries. These standards specifically address the requirements for 
compounding equipment and facilities. ISO 5 (class 100) certification is 
required for the direct compounding of sterile products, while ISO 7 (class 
10,000) certificatio n applies to the surrounding clean room environment.  
 
 
 

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual      Sanitizing and Maintenance  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page K -5 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  The DRIPBaR Standard and USP 797  
DRIPBa R follows USP 797. USP 797 is the first set of enforceable 
sterile  compounding standards issued by the United States Pharmacop oeia 
(USP). USP 797 describes the guidelines, procedures, and compliance 
requirements for  compounding sterile preparations and sets the 
standards  that apply to all settings in which sterile preparations 
are compounded.  
The writing of chapter 797 by the USP c ame after decades of increasing 
safety and  quality  consciousness. Compounding medications has always 
been and continues to be a high -risk, labor -intensive  process. Several 
factors can contribute to  improper  compounding or non -sterile preparations, 
both of which can be a cause of death or injury in clients . Studies have 
provided ample evidence of  the prevalence of pharmacies' compounding 
errors,  and there have been isolated published reports of  client  death and 
injury a ssociated with sterile  preparations.  The implementation of USP 797 
ensures  that pharmacies will not overlook problems or  evade  compliance 
with necessary provisions.  
Client Safety:  Inserting a catheter into a vein exposes clients to the 
possibility of infection. If there is any contamination from bacterial or fungal 
sources within  the infusion, it can be transmitted to the client, with 
potentially fatal consequences.  
The DRIPBaR uses the highest standard of sterile compounding to minimize 
the risk of infection.  
Client Confidence:  As infusion medicine continues to proliferate, people will 
make their  choice based on their confidence in the centre . 
The DRIPBaR has  established a reputation for being a leader in safety. We 
are the first  national franchise to utilize and follow 797 standards at all 
locations. This is best practice and in turn, best for business. The more 
people  understand the difference , the more they will choose The DRIPBaR.  
Protection from Administrative Consequence:  The business of infusion -
based healthcare is moving faster than  state regulatory agencies can catch 
up to.  
In the years to come,  this imbalance will be corrected, and infusion centers 
nationwide will  come under provincial  scrutiny and inspection. If they have 
not been following USP 797 standards, they risk negative consequences like 
being shut down.  

DRIPBaR Franchising, LLC  
Confidential Franchise Operations Manual      Sanitizing and Maintenance  
Proprietary and Confidential Information   Page K -6 
Version Number: 1.0 January 2025  Medical and Legal Protection:  In the event of an adverse consequence such 
as an infection to a  client, not having a sterile hood  or not complying with 
USP 797  standards make any infusion centre  difficult to defend. In such 
a circumstance, the infusion centre , its Medical Director, and its Nurses 
would all be potentially liable.  
General Chapter 797 sets practice standards for compounding sterile 
preparations to ensure that all client s receive sterile, safe, and accurate 
preparations during treatment.
